<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn,_Earth_size_comparison.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2601" data-file-width="3000" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Saturn%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg/220px-Saturn%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Saturn%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg/330px-Saturn%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Saturn%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg/440px-Saturn%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn,_Earth_size_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Composite image comparing the sizes of Saturn and Earth</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Saturn is a <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giant</a> because it is predominantly composed of hydrogen and helium. It lacks a definite surface, though it may have a solid core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-melosh2011_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-melosh2011-21">[19]</a></sup> Saturn's rotation causes it to have the shape of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oblate_spheroid" title="Oblate spheroid">oblate spheroid</a>; that is, it is flattened at the poles and bulges at its equator. Its equatorial and polar radii differ by almost 10%: 60,268 km versus 54,364 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-fact-5">[5]</a></sup> Jupiter, <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, and Neptune, the other giant planets in the Solar System, are also oblate but to a lesser extent. Saturn is the only planet of the Solar System that is less dense than water—about 30% less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-preserve_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-preserve-22">[20]</a></sup> Although Saturn's <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">core</a> is considerably denser than water, the average <a href="/wiki/Relative_density" title="Relative density">specific density</a> of the planet is 0.69 g/cm<sup>3</sup> due to the atmosphere. Jupiter has 318 times the Earth's mass,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jupiter_fact_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jupiter_fact-23">[21]</a></sup> and Saturn is 95 times the mass of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-fact-5">[5]</a></sup> Together, Jupiter and Saturn hold 92% of the total planetary mass in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssr152_1_423_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-ssr152_1_423-24">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 8 January 2015, NASA reported that a team of scientists from the Jet Propulsion Laboratory determined the <a href="/wiki/Barycenter" title="Barycenter">barycenter</a> of the planet Saturn and its <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Saturn" title="Moons of Saturn">family of moons</a> to within 4 km (2.5 mi) using data from an experiment conducted with the Cassini spacecraft and the Very Large Baseline Array.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150108_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150108-25">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">Internal structure</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1600" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Saturn_diagram.svg/220px-Saturn_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Saturn_diagram.svg/330px-Saturn_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Saturn_diagram.svg/440px-Saturn_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diagram of Saturn, to scale</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite consisting mostly of hydrogen and helium, most of Saturn's mass is not in the <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> <a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">phase</a>, because hydrogen becomes a <a href="/wiki/Ideal_solution" title="Ideal solution">non-ideal liquid</a> when the density is above 0.01 g/cm<sup>3</sup>, which is reached at a radius containing 99.9% of Saturn's mass. The temperature, pressure, and density inside Saturn all rise steadily toward the core, which causes hydrogen to be a metal in the deeper layers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssr152_1_423_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-ssr152_1_423-24">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Standard planetary models suggest that the interior of Saturn is similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, having a small rocky core surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> with trace amounts of various <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">volatiles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guillot_et_al2009_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-guillot_et_al2009-26">[24]</a></sup> This core is similar in composition to the Earth, but more dense. Examination of Saturn's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_moment" title="Gravitational moment">gravitational moment</a>, in combination with physical models of the interior, has allowed constraints to be placed on the mass of Saturn's core. In 2004, scientists estimated that the core must be 9–22 times the mass of the Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science305_5689_1414_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-science305_5689_1414-27">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj609_2_1170_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj609_2_1170-28">[26]</a></sup> which corresponds to a diameter of about 25,000 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup> This is surrounded by a thicker liquid <a href="/wiki/Metallic_hydrogen" title="Metallic hydrogen">metallic hydrogen</a> layer, followed by a liquid layer of helium-saturated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_hydrogen" title="Molecular hydrogen">molecular hydrogen</a> that gradually transitions to a gas with increasing altitude. The outermost layer spans 1,000 km and consists of gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faure_mensing2007_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-faure_mensing2007-30">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMM_Saturn_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-NMM_Saturn-31">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Saturn has a hot interior, reaching 11,700 °C at its core, and it radiates 2.5 times more energy into space than it receives from the Sun. Jupiter's <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> is generated by the <a href="/wiki/Kelvin%E2%80%93Helmholtz_mechanism" title="Kelvin–Helmholtz mechanism">Kelvin–Helmholtz mechanism</a> of slow <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_compression" title="Gravitational compression">gravitational compression</a>, but such a process alone may not be sufficient to explain heat production for Saturn, because it is less massive. An alternative or additional mechanism may be generation of heat through the "raining out" of droplets of helium deep in Saturn's interior. As the droplets descend through the lower-density hydrogen, the process releases heat by <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a> and leaves Saturn's outer layers depleted of helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_pater_lissauer2010_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-de_pater_lissauer2010-33">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_saturn_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_saturn-34">[32]</a></sup> These descending droplets may have accumulated into a helium shell surrounding the core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guillot_et_al2009_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-guillot_et_al2009-26">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">Rainfalls</a> of <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamonds</a> have been suggested to occur on Saturn, as well as on Jupiter<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SC-20131009_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-SC-20131009-35">[33]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giants</a> <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP-20170825_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-WP-20170825-36">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">Atmosphere</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18354-Saturn-MethaneBands-20150906.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1020" data-file-width="1020" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/PIA18354-Saturn-MethaneBands-20150906.jpg/220px-PIA18354-Saturn-MethaneBands-20150906.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/PIA18354-Saturn-MethaneBands-20150906.jpg/330px-PIA18354-Saturn-MethaneBands-20150906.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/PIA18354-Saturn-MethaneBands-20150906.jpg/440px-PIA18354-Saturn-MethaneBands-20150906.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18354-Saturn-MethaneBands-20150906.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a> bands circle Saturn. The moon Dione hangs below the rings to the right.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The outer atmosphere of Saturn contains 96.3% molecular hydrogen and 3.25% helium by volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> The proportion of helium is significantly deficient compared to the abundance of this element in the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guillot_et_al2009_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-guillot_et_al2009-26">[24]</a></sup> The quantity of elements heavier than helium (<a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metallicity</a>) is not known precisely, but the proportions are assumed to match the primordial abundances from the formation of the Solar System. The total mass of these heavier elements is estimated to be 19–31 times the mass of the Earth, with a significant fraction located in Saturn's core region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science286_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-science286-38">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Trace amounts of ammonia, <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphine" title="Phosphine">phosphine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> have been detected in Saturn's atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baas15_831_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-baas15_831-39">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cain2009_24029_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-cain2009_24029-40">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pfsaa2008_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-pfsaa2008-41">[39]</a></sup> The upper clouds are composed of ammonia crystals, while the lower level clouds appear to consist of either <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_hydrosulfide" title="Ammonium hydrosulfide">ammonium hydrosulfide</a> (NH<sub>4</sub>SH) or water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-martinez20050905_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-martinez20050905-42">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">Ultraviolet</a> radiation from the Sun causes methane <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photolysis" title="Photolysis">photolysis</a> in the upper atmosphere, leading to a series of <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> chemical reactions with the resulting products being carried downward by eddies and diffusion. This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photochemical_cycle" title="Photochemical cycle">photochemical cycle</a> is modulated by Saturn's annual seasonal cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pfsaa2008_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-pfsaa2008-41">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cloud_layers">Cloud layers</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_Storm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1015" data-file-width="1015" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Saturn_Storm.jpg/220px-Saturn_Storm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Saturn_Storm.jpg/330px-Saturn_Storm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Saturn_Storm.jpg/440px-Saturn_Storm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_Storm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A global storm girdles the planet in 2011. The head of the storm (bright area) passes the tail circling around the left limb.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Saturn's atmosphere exhibits a banded pattern similar to Jupiter's, but Saturn's bands are much fainter and are much wider near the equator. The nomenclature used to describe these bands is the same as on Jupiter. Saturn's finer cloud patterns were not observed until the flybys of the <i>Voyager</i> spacecraft during the 1980s. Since then, Earth-based <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescopy</a> has improved to the point where regular observations can be made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emp105_2_143_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-emp105_2_143-43">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>The composition of the clouds varies with depth and increasing pressure. In the upper cloud layers, with the temperature in the range 100–160 K and pressures extending between 0.5–2 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bar</a>, the clouds consist of ammonia ice. Water <a href="/wiki/Ice_cloud" title="Ice cloud">ice clouds</a> begin at a level where the pressure is about 2.5 bar and extend down to 9.5 bar, where temperatures range from 185–270 K. Intermixed in this layer is a band of ammonium hydrosulfide ice, lying in the pressure range 3–6 bar with temperatures of 190–235 K. Finally, the lower layers, where pressures are between 10–20 bar and temperatures are 270–330 K, contains a region of water droplets with ammonia in aqueous solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dougherty_esposito2009_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-dougherty_esposito2009-44">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Saturn's usually bland atmosphere occasionally exhibits long-lived ovals and other features common on Jupiter. In 1990, the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> imaged an enormous white cloud near Saturn's equator that was not present during the <i>Voyager</i> encounters, and in 1994 another smaller storm was observed. The 1990 storm was an example of a <a href="/wiki/Great_White_Spot" title="Great White Spot">Great White Spot</a>, a unique but short-lived phenomenon that occurs once every Saturnian year, roughly every 30 Earth years, around the time of the northern hemisphere's <a href="/wiki/Summer_solstice" title="Summer solstice">summer solstice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icarus176_1_155_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-icarus176_1_155-45">[43]</a></sup> Previous Great White Spots were observed in 1876, 1903, 1933 and 1960, with the 1933 storm being the most famous. If the periodicity is maintained, another storm will occur in about 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>The winds on Saturn are the second fastest among the Solar System's planets, after Neptune's. <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a> data indicate peak easterly winds of 500 m/s (1800 km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_Summary_1_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Voyager_Summary_1-47">[45]</a></sup> In images from the <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini</a></i> spacecraft during 2007, Saturn's northern hemisphere displayed a bright blue hue, similar to Uranus. The color was most likely caused by <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering" title="Rayleigh scattering">Rayleigh scattering</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thermography" title="Thermography">Thermography</a> has shown that Saturn's south pole has a warm <a href="/wiki/Polar_vortex" title="Polar vortex">polar vortex</a>, the only known example of such a phenomenon in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCP_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-MCP-49">[47]</a></sup> Whereas temperatures on Saturn are normally −185 °C, temperatures on the vortex often reach as high as −122 °C, suspected to be the warmest spot on Saturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCP_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-MCP-49">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_pole_hexagonal_cloud_pattern">North pole hexagonal cloud pattern</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rotatingsaturnhexagon.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="384" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Rotatingsaturnhexagon.gif/220px-Rotatingsaturnhexagon.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Rotatingsaturnhexagon.gif/330px-Rotatingsaturnhexagon.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bd/Rotatingsaturnhexagon.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rotatingsaturnhexagon.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Saturn's north pole (<a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">IR</a>;animation)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Saturn%27s_hexagon" title="Saturn's hexagon">Saturn's hexagon</a></div>
<p>A persisting <a href="/wiki/Hexagon" title="Hexagon">hexagonal</a> wave pattern around the north polar vortex in the atmosphere at about 78°N was first noted in the Voyager images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20140806_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20140806-52">[50]</a></sup> The sides of the hexagon are each about 13,800 km (8,600 mi) long, which is longer than the diameter of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hexagon_ref_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hexagon_ref-53">[51]</a></sup> The entire structure rotates with a period of <span class="nowrap">10h 39m 24s</span> (the same period as that of the planet's radio emissions) which is assumed to be equal to the period of rotation of Saturn's interior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science247_4947_1206_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-science247_4947_1206-54">[52]</a></sup> The hexagonal feature does not shift in longitude like the other clouds in the visible atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pss57_14_1671_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-pss57_14_1671-55">[53]</a></sup> The pattern's origin is a matter of much speculation. Most scientists think it is a <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave" title="Standing wave">standing wave</a> pattern in the atmosphere. Polygonal shapes have been replicated in the laboratory through differential rotation of fluids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-labmodel_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-labmodel-57">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_pole_vortex">South pole vortex</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Looking_saturn_in_the_eye.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="560" data-file-width="540" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Looking_saturn_in_the_eye.jpg/220px-Looking_saturn_in_the_eye.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Looking_saturn_in_the_eye.jpg/330px-Looking_saturn_in_the_eye.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Looking_saturn_in_the_eye.jpg/440px-Looking_saturn_in_the_eye.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Looking_saturn_in_the_eye.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Saturn's south pole</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">HST</a> imaging of the south polar region indicates the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Jet_stream" title="Jet stream">jet stream</a>, but no strong polar vortex nor any hexagonal standing wave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> reported in November 2006 that <i>Cassini</i> had observed a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hurricane" title="Hurricane">hurricane</a>-like" storm locked to the south pole that had a clearly defined <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eyewall" title="Eyewall">eyewall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> Eyewall clouds had not previously been seen on any planet other than Earth. For example, images from the <i><a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">Galileo</a></i> spacecraft did not show an eyewall in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">Great Red Spot</a> of Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>The south pole storm may have been present for billions of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spole_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-spole-62">[60]</a></sup> This vortex is comparable to the size of Earth, and it has winds of 550 km/h.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spole_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-spole-62">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_features">Other features</span></h4>
<p><i>Cassini</i> has observed a series of cloud features nicknamed "String of Pearls" found in northern latitudes. These features are cloud clearings that reside in deeper cloud layers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetosphere">Magnetosphere</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere_of_Saturn" title="Magnetosphere of Saturn">Magnetosphere of Saturn</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn%27s_double_aurorae_(captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1024" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Saturn%27s_double_aurorae_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.jpg/220px-Saturn%27s_double_aurorae_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Saturn%27s_double_aurorae_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.jpg/330px-Saturn%27s_double_aurorae_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Saturn%27s_double_aurorae_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.jpg/440px-Saturn%27s_double_aurorae_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn%27s_double_aurorae_(captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Polar aurorae on Saturn</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_sees_a_flickering_light_display_on_Saturn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2222" data-file-width="3519" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Hubble_sees_a_flickering_light_display_on_Saturn.jpg/220px-Hubble_sees_a_flickering_light_display_on_Saturn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Hubble_sees_a_flickering_light_display_on_Saturn.jpg/330px-Hubble_sees_a_flickering_light_display_on_Saturn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Hubble_sees_a_flickering_light_display_on_Saturn.jpg/440px-Hubble_sees_a_flickering_light_display_on_Saturn.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_sees_a_flickering_light_display_on_Saturn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Auroral lights at Saturn’s north pole<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Saturn has an intrinsic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic" title="Magnetic">magnetic</a> field that has a simple, symmetric shape – a magnetic <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">dipole</a>. Its strength at the equator – 0.2 <a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">gauss</a> (20 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microtesla" title="Microtesla">µT</a>) – is approximately one twentieth of that of the field around Jupiter and slightly weaker than Earth's <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mag_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-mag-17">[15]</a></sup> As a result, Saturn's <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> is much smaller than Jupiter's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mag_2_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-mag_2-65">[63]</a></sup> When <i>Voyager 2</i> entered the magnetosphere, the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> pressure was high and the magnetosphere extended only 19 Saturn radii, or 1.1 million km (712,000 mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup> although it enlarged within several hours, and remained so for about three days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atkinson2010_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-atkinson2010-67">[65]</a></sup> Most probably, the magnetic field is generated similarly to that of Jupiter – by currents in the liquid metallic-hydrogen layer called a metallic-hydrogen dynamo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mag_2_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-mag_2-65">[63]</a></sup> This magnetosphere is efficient at deflecting the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> particles from the Sun. The moon Titan orbits within the outer part of Saturn's magnetosphere and contributes plasma from the <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionized</a> particles in Titan's outer atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mag_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-mag-17">[15]</a></sup> Saturn's magnetosphere, like <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's</a>, produces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">aurorae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_rotation">Orbit and rotation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA20517-Saturn%26Rings-CassiniSpacecraftScene-20161028.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1020" data-file-width="1020" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/PIA20517-Saturn%26Rings-CassiniSpacecraftScene-20161028.jpg/220px-PIA20517-Saturn%26Rings-CassiniSpacecraftScene-20161028.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/PIA20517-Saturn%26Rings-CassiniSpacecraftScene-20161028.jpg/330px-PIA20517-Saturn%26Rings-CassiniSpacecraftScene-20161028.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/PIA20517-Saturn%26Rings-CassiniSpacecraftScene-20161028.jpg/440px-PIA20517-Saturn%26Rings-CassiniSpacecraftScene-20161028.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA20517-Saturn%26Rings-CassiniSpacecraftScene-20161028.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Saturn and rings as viewed by the <i>Cassini</i> spacecraft (28 October 2016)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The average distance between Saturn and the Sun is over 1.4 billion kilometers (9 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>). With an average orbital speed of 9.68 km/s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-fact-5">[5]</a></sup> it takes Saturn 10,759 Earth days (or about <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap">29<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> years),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cain2009_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-cain2009-69">[67]</a></sup> to finish one revolution around the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-fact-5">[5]</a></sup> The elliptical orbit of Saturn is inclined 2.48° relative to the orbital plane of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_5-8"><a href="#cite_note-fact-5">[5]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">perihelion and aphelion</a> distances are, respectively, 9.195 and 9.957 AU, on average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_5-9"><a href="#cite_note-fact-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup> The visible features on Saturn rotate at different rates depending on latitude and multiple rotation periods have been assigned to various regions (as in Jupiter's case).</p>
<p>Astronomers use three different systems for specifying the rotation rate of Saturn. <i>System I</i> has a period of 10 hr 14 min 00 sec (844.3°/d) and encompasses the Equatorial Zone, the South Equatorial Belt and the North Equatorial Belt. The polar regions are considered to have rotation rates similar to <i>System I</i>. All other Saturnian latitudes, excluding the north and south polar regions, are indicated as <i>System II</i> and have been assigned a rotation period of 10 hr 38 min 25.4 sec (810.76°/d). <i>System III</i> refers to Saturn's internal rotation rate. Based on <a href="/wiki/Radio_astronomy" title="Radio astronomy">radio</a> emissions from the planet in the period of the Voyager flybys, it has been assigned a rotation period of 10 hr 39 min 22.4 sec (810.8°/d). Because it is close to System II, it has largely superseded it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benton2006_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-benton2006-71">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>A precise value for the rotation period of the interior remains elusive. While approaching Saturn in 2004, <i>Cassini</i> found that the radio rotation period of Saturn had increased appreciably, to approximately 10 hr 45 min 45 sec (± 36 sec).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cain2008_15298_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-cain2008_15298-73">[71]</a></sup> The latest estimate of Saturn's rotation (as an indicated rotation rate for Saturn as a whole) based on a compilation of various measurements from the <i>Cassini</i>, <i>Voyager</i> and Pioneer probes was reported in September 2007 is 10 hr 32 min 35 sec.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson2007_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson2007-74">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2007, it was found that the variation of radio emissions from the planet did not match Saturn's rotation rate. This variance may be caused by geyser activity on Saturn's moon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">Enceladus</a>. The water vapor emitted into Saturn's orbit by this activity becomes charged and creates a drag upon Saturn's magnetic field, slowing its rotation slightly relative to the rotation of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science316_5823_442_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-science316_5823_442-76">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science316_5823_380_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-science316_5823_380-77">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_satellites">Natural satellites</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Saturn" title="Moons of Saturn">Moons of Saturn</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_System_Montage_-_GPN-2000-000439.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2015" data-file-width="2351" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Saturn_System_Montage_-_GPN-2000-000439.jpg/220px-Saturn_System_Montage_-_GPN-2000-000439.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Saturn_System_Montage_-_GPN-2000-000439.jpg/330px-Saturn_System_Montage_-_GPN-2000-000439.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Saturn_System_Montage_-_GPN-2000-000439.jpg/440px-Saturn_System_Montage_-_GPN-2000-000439.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_System_Montage_-_GPN-2000-000439.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A montage of Saturn and its principal <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a> (<a href="/wiki/Dione_(moon)" title="Dione (moon)">Dione</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(moon)" title="Tethys (moon)">Tethys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">Mimas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">Enceladus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">Rhea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>; <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)" title="Iapetus (moon)">Iapetus</a> not shown). This famous image was created from photographs taken in November 1980 by the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> spacecraft.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Saturn has 62 known <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a>, 53 of which have formal names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPLmoons_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-JPLmoons-78">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup> In addition, there is evidence of dozens to hundreds of moonlets with diameters of 40–500 meters in Saturn's rings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup> which are not considered to be true moons. <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, the largest moon, comprises more than 90% of the mass in orbit around Saturn, including the rings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brunier2005_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-brunier2005-81">[79]</a></sup> Saturn's second-largest moon, <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">Rhea</a>, may have a tenuous <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Rhea" title="Rings of Rhea">ring system of its own</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2008_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2008-82">[80]</a></sup> along with a tenuous <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atkinson20101126_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-atkinson20101126-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ryan20101126_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-ryan20101126-85">[83]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="496" data-file-width="496" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg/220px-PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg/330px-PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg/440px-PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Possible beginning of a new moon (white dot) of Saturn (image taken by <i>Cassini</i> on 15 April 2013)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Many of the other moons are small: 34 are less than 10 km in diameter and another 14 between 10 and 50 km in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saturn_moons_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saturn_moons-86">[84]</a></sup> Traditionally, most of Saturn's moons have been named after <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titans</a> of Greek mythology. Titan is the only satellite in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> with a major <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Titan" title="Atmosphere of Titan">atmosphere</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup> in which a complex organic chemistry occurs. It is the only satellite with <a href="/wiki/Lakes_of_Titan" title="Lakes of Titan">hydrocarbon lakes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 6 June 2013, scientists at the <a href="/wiki/Instituto_de_Astrof%C3%ADsica_de_Andaluc%C3%ADa" title="Instituto de Astrofísica de Andalucía">IAA-CSIC</a> reported the detection of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbons" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_atmosphere" title="Upper atmosphere">upper atmosphere</a> of Titan, a <a href="/wiki/PAH_world_hypothesis" title="PAH world hypothesis">possible precursor for life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAA-20130606_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAA-20130606-91">[89]</a></sup> On 23 June 2014, NASA claimed to have strong evidence that <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> in the atmosphere of Titan came from materials in the <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a>, associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comets" title="Comets">comets</a>, and not from the materials that formed Saturn in earlier times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-201450623_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-201450623-92">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Saturn's moon <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">Enceladus</a>, which seems similar in chemical makeup to comets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NS-20080326_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-NS-20080326-93">[91]</a></sup> has often been regarded as a potential <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitat</a> for <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microbial life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-madrigal20090624_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-madrigal20090624-95">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotts20050928_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-spotts20050928-96">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup> Evidence of this possibility includes the satellite's salt-rich particles having an "ocean-like" composition that indicates most of Enceladus's expelled <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a> comes from the evaporation of liquid salt water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup> A 2015 flyby by <i>Cassini</i> through a plume on Enceladus found most of the ingredients to sustain life forms that live by methanogenesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20170413_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20170413-101">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2014, NASA scientists reported the possible beginning of a new moon within the <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#A_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">A Ring</a>, which was imaged by <i>Cassini</i> on 15 April 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140414a_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140414a-102">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planetary_rings">Planetary rings</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">Rings of Saturn</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_from_Cassini_Orbiter_(2007-01-19).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2908" data-file-width="4088" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Saturn_from_Cassini_Orbiter_%282007-01-19%29.jpg/220px-Saturn_from_Cassini_Orbiter_%282007-01-19%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Saturn_from_Cassini_Orbiter_%282007-01-19%29.jpg/330px-Saturn_from_Cassini_Orbiter_%282007-01-19%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Saturn_from_Cassini_Orbiter_%282007-01-19%29.jpg/440px-Saturn_from_Cassini_Orbiter_%282007-01-19%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">rings of Saturn</a> (imaged here by <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini</a></i> in 2007) are the most massive and conspicuous in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMM_Saturn_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-NMM_Saturn-31">[29]</a></sup></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn%27s_A_Ring_From_the_Inside_Out.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="482" data-file-width="691" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Saturn%27s_A_Ring_From_the_Inside_Out.jpg/220px-Saturn%27s_A_Ring_From_the_Inside_Out.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Saturn%27s_A_Ring_From_the_Inside_Out.jpg/330px-Saturn%27s_A_Ring_From_the_Inside_Out.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Saturn%27s_A_Ring_From_the_Inside_Out.jpg/440px-Saturn%27s_A_Ring_From_the_Inside_Out.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">False-color <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV" title="UV">UV</a> image of Saturn's outer <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#B_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">B</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#A_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">A</a> rings; dirtier ringlets in the <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#Cassini_Division" title="Rings of Saturn">Cassini Division</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#Encke_Gap" title="Rings of Saturn">Encke Gap</a> show up red.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Saturn is probably best known for the system of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">planetary rings</a> that makes it visually unique.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMM_Saturn_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-NMM_Saturn-31">[29]</a></sup> The rings extend from 6,630 km to 120,700 km outward from Saturn's equator, average approximately 20 meters in thickness and are composed of 93% water ice with traces of <a href="/wiki/Tholin" title="Tholin">tholin</a> impurities and 7% amorphous <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup> The particles that make up the rings range in size from specks of dust up to 10 m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup> While the other <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giants</a> also have ring systems, Saturn's is the largest and most visible.</p>
<p>There are two main hypotheses regarding the origin of the rings. One hypothesis is that the rings are remnants of a destroyed moon of Saturn. The second hypothesis is that the rings are left over from the original <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebular</a> material from which Saturn formed. Some ice in the E ring comes from the moon Enceladus's geysers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spahn_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spahn-105">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the past, astronomers once thought the rings formed alongside the planet when it formed billions of years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup> Instead, the age of these planetary rings is probably some hundreds of millions of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beyond the main rings at a distance of 12 million km from the planet is the sparse Phoebe ring, which is tilted at an angle of 27° to the other rings and, like <a href="/wiki/Phoebe_(moon)" title="Phoebe (moon)">Phoebe</a>, orbits in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" title="Retrograde motion">retrograde</a> fashion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some of the moons of Saturn, including <a href="/wiki/Pandora_(moon)" title="Pandora (moon)">Pandora</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prometheus_(moon)" title="Prometheus (moon)">Prometheus</a>, act as shepherd moons to confine the rings and prevent them from spreading out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russell2004_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-russell2004-111">[109]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pan_(moon)" title="Pan (moon)">Pan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(moon)" title="Atlas (moon)">Atlas</a> cause weak, linear density waves in Saturn's rings that have yielded more reliable calculations of their masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_observation_and_exploration">History of observation and exploration</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Saturn" title="Exploration of Saturn">Exploration of Saturn</a></div>
<p>There have been three main phases in the observation and exploration of Saturn. The first era was ancient observations (such as with the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a>), before the invention of the modern telescopes. Starting in the 17th century progressively more advanced telescopic observations from Earth have been made. The other type is visitation by spacecraft, either by orbiting or flyby. In the 21st century observations continue from the Earth (or Earth-orbiting observatories) and from the Cassini orbiter at Saturn.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_observations">Ancient observations</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(mythology)" title="Saturn (mythology)">Saturn (mythology)</a></div>
<p>Saturn has been known since prehistoric times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMM_Saturn_2_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-NMM_Saturn_2-113">[111]</a></sup> In ancient times, it was the most distant of the known planets in the Solar System and thus a major character in various mythologies. <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_astronomy" title="Babylonian astronomy">Babylonian astronomers</a> systematically observed and recorded the movements of Saturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ptrsl276_1257_43_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-ptrsl276_1257_43-114">[112]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">ancient Roman mythology</a>, the god <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(mythology)" title="Saturn (mythology)">Saturnus</a>, from which the planet takes its name, was the god of agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saturn_in_ancient_mythologies_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saturn_in_ancient_mythologies-115">[113]</a></sup> The Romans considered Saturnus the equivalent of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek god</a> <a href="/wiki/Cronus" title="Cronus">Cronus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saturn_in_ancient_mythologies_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Saturn_in_ancient_mythologies-115">[113]</a></sup> The Greeks had made the outermost planet sacred to Cronus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evans1998_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-evans1998-116">[114]</a></sup> and the Romans followed suit. (In modern <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, the planet retains its ancient name <i>Cronus</i>—Κρόνος: <i>Kronos</i>.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Greek scientist <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> based his calculations of Saturn's orbit on observations he made while it was in <a href="/wiki/Opposition_(planets)" title="Opposition (planets)">opposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ps04_1893_862_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-ps04_1893_862-118">[116]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Jyotisha" title="Jyotisha">Hindu astrology</a>, there are nine astrological objects, known as <a href="/wiki/Navagraha" title="Navagraha">Navagrahas</a>. Saturn is known as "<a href="/wiki/Shani" title="Shani">Shani</a>" and judges everyone based on the good and bad deeds performed in life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saturn_in_ancient_mythologies_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-Saturn_in_ancient_mythologies-115">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ps04_1893_862_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-ps04_1893_862-118">[116]</a></sup> Ancient <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astrology" title="Chinese astrology">Chinese</a> and Japanese culture designated the planet Saturn as the "earth star" (<span lang="zh">土星</span>). This was based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_elements_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Five elements (Chinese philosophy)">Five Elements</a> which were traditionally used to classify natural elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>In ancient <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>, Saturn is called 'Shabbathai'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup> Its angel is <a href="/wiki/Cassiel" title="Cassiel">Cassiel</a>. Its intelligence or beneficial spirit is <a href="/wiki/Agiel" title="Agiel">Agiel</a> (layga) and its spirit (darker aspect) is Zazel (lzaz). In <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language" title="Ottoman Turkish language">Ottoman Turkish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay</a>, its name is 'Zuhal', derived from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> زحل.</p>
<h3><span id="European_observations_(17th–19th_centuries)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="European_observations_.2817th.E2.80.9319th_centuries.29">European observations (17th–19th centuries)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_Robert_Hooke_1666.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="594" data-file-width="886" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Saturn_Robert_Hooke_1666.jpg/220px-Saturn_Robert_Hooke_1666.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Saturn_Robert_Hooke_1666.jpg/330px-Saturn_Robert_Hooke_1666.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Saturn_Robert_Hooke_1666.jpg/440px-Saturn_Robert_Hooke_1666.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_Robert_Hooke_1666.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a> noted the shadows (a and b) cast by both the globe and the rings on each other in this drawing of Saturn in 1666.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Saturn's rings require at least a 15-mm-diameter <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-binoculars_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-binoculars-123">[121]</a></sup> to resolve and thus were not known to exist until <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo" title="Galileo">Galileo</a> first saw them in 1610.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chan2000_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-chan2000-124">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cain2008_15390_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-cain2008_15390-125">[123]</a></sup> He thought of them as two moons on Saturn's sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cain2008_15418_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-cain2008_15418-126">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cain2008_46237_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-cain2008_46237-127">[125]</a></sup> It was not until <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a> used greater telescopic magnification that this notion was refuted. Huygens discovered Saturn's moon Titan; <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Domenico_Cassini" title="Giovanni Domenico Cassini">Giovanni Domenico Cassini</a> later discovered four other moons: <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)" title="Iapetus (moon)">Iapetus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">Rhea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(moon)" title="Tethys (moon)">Tethys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dione_(moon)" title="Dione (moon)">Dione</a>. In 1675, Cassini discovered the gap now known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cassini_Division" title="Cassini Division">Cassini Division</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-micek2007_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-micek2007-128">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>No further discoveries of significance were made until 1789 when <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a> discovered two further moons, <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">Mimas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">Enceladus</a>. The irregularly shaped satellite <a href="/wiki/Hyperion_(moon)" title="Hyperion (moon)">Hyperion</a>, which has a <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">resonance</a> with Titan, was discovered in 1848 by a British team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pa54_122_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-pa54_122-129">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1899 <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Pickering" title="William Henry Pickering">William Henry Pickering</a> discovered Phoebe, a highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irregular_satellite" title="Irregular satellite">irregular satellite</a> that does not rotate synchronously with Saturn as the larger moons do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pa54_122_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-pa54_122-129">[127]</a></sup> Phoebe was the first such satellite found and it takes more than a year to orbit Saturn in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_orbit" title="Retrograde orbit">retrograde orbit</a>. During the early 20th century, research on Titan led to the confirmation in 1944 that it had a thick atmosphere – a feature unique among the Solar System's moons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj100_378_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj100_378-130">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_NASA_and_ESA_probes">Modern NASA and ESA probes</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pioneer_11_flyby"><i>Pioneer 11</i> flyby</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P11saturnb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="373" data-file-width="646" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/P11saturnb.jpg/220px-P11saturnb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/P11saturnb.jpg/330px-P11saturnb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/P11saturnb.jpg/440px-P11saturnb.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:P11saturnb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Pioneer 11</i> image of Saturn</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_11" title="Pioneer 11">Pioneer 11</a></i> made the first flyby of Saturn in September 1979, when it passed within 20,000 km of the planet's cloud tops. Images were taken of the planet and a few of its moons, although their resolution was too low to discern surface detail. The spacecraft also studied Saturn's rings, revealing the thin F-ring and the fact that dark gaps in the rings are bright when viewed at high <a href="/wiki/Phase_angle_(astronomy)" title="Phase angle (astronomy)">phase angle</a> (towards the Sun), meaning that they contain fine light-scattering material. In addition, Pioneer 11 measured the temperature of Titan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Voyager_flybys">Voyager flybys</span></h4>
<p>In November 1980, the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> probe visited the Saturn system. It sent back the first high-resolution images of the planet, its rings and satellites. Surface features of various moons were seen for the first time. <i>Voyager 1</i> performed a close flyby of Titan, increasing knowledge of the atmosphere of the moon. It proved that Titan's atmosphere is impenetrable in visible wavelengths; therefore no surface details were seen. The flyby changed the spacecraft's trajectory out from the plane of the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Voyager-132">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>Almost a year later, in August 1981, <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> continued the study of the Saturn system. More close-up images of Saturn's moons were acquired, as well as evidence of changes in the atmosphere and the rings. Unfortunately, during the flyby, the probe's turnable camera platform stuck for a couple of days and some planned imaging was lost. Saturn's gravity was used to direct the spacecraft's trajectory towards Uranus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Voyager-132">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>The probes discovered and confirmed several new satellites orbiting near or within the planet's rings, as well as the small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maxwell_Gap" title="Maxwell Gap">Maxwell Gap</a> (a gap within the <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#C_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">C Ring</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keeler_gap" title="Keeler gap">Keeler gap</a> (a 42 km wide gap in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Ring" title="A Ring">A Ring</a>).</p>
<h4><span id="Cassini–Huygens_spacecraft"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cassini.E2.80.93Huygens_spacecraft"><i>Cassini–Huygens</i> spacecraft</span></h4>
<div class="mw-stack mobile-float-reset" style="box-sizing: border-box; overflow: hidden; float:right; margin:1px;">
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earth-Moon_system_as_seen_from_Saturn_(PIA17171).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Earth-Moon_system_as_seen_from_Saturn_%28PIA17171%29.jpg/220px-Earth-Moon_system_as_seen_from_Saturn_%28PIA17171%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Earth-Moon_system_as_seen_from_Saturn_%28PIA17171%29.jpg/330px-Earth-Moon_system_as_seen_from_Saturn_%28PIA17171%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Earth-Moon_system_as_seen_from_Saturn_%28PIA17171%29.jpg/440px-Earth-Moon_system_as_seen_from_Saturn_%28PIA17171%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earth-Moon_system_as_seen_from_Saturn_(PIA17171).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cassini_spacecraft" title="Cassini spacecraft"><i>Cassini</i></a> (19 July 2013).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earth-AsSeenFromSaturn-20170412.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="374" data-file-width="374" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Earth-AsSeenFromSaturn-20170412.jpg/220px-Earth-AsSeenFromSaturn-20170412.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Earth-AsSeenFromSaturn-20170412.jpg/330px-Earth-AsSeenFromSaturn-20170412.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4d/Earth-AsSeenFromSaturn-20170412.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earth-AsSeenFromSaturn-20170412.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> viewed from the rings of Saturn (12 April 2017)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 1 July 2004, the <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini–Huygens</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probe</a> performed the SOI (Saturn Orbit Insertion) maneuver and entered orbit around Saturn. Before the SOI, <i>Cassini</i> had already studied the system extensively. In June 2004, it had conducted a close flyby of <a href="/wiki/Phoebe_(moon)" title="Phoebe (moon)">Phoebe</a>, sending back high-resolution images and data. <i>Cassini</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> flyby of Saturn's largest moon, Titan, has captured radar images of large lakes and their coastlines with numerous islands and mountains. The orbiter completed two Titan flybys before releasing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huygens_probe" title="Huygens probe"><i>Huygens</i> probe</a> on 25 December 2004. <i>Huygens</i> descended onto the surface of Titan on 14 January 2005, sending a flood of data during the atmospheric descent and after the landing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature438_7069_758_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature438_7069_758-133">[131]</a></sup> <i>Cassini</i> has since conducted multiple flybys of Titan and other icy satellites.</p>
<p>Starting in early 2005, scientists used <i>Cassini</i> to track lightning on Saturn. The power of the lightning is approximately 1,000 times that of lightning on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_geysers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="977" data-file-width="1580" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Enceladus_geysers.jpg/220px-Enceladus_geysers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Enceladus_geysers.jpg/330px-Enceladus_geysers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Enceladus_geysers.jpg/440px-Enceladus_geysers.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_geysers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
At Enceladus's south pole geysers spray water from many locations along the <a href="/wiki/Tiger_stripes_(Enceladus)" title="Tiger stripes (Enceladus)">tiger stripes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140728_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140728-135">[133]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2006, NASA reported that <i>Cassini</i> had found evidence of liquid water reservoirs that erupt in <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">geysers</a> on Saturn's moon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">Enceladus</a>. Images had shown jets of icy particles being emitted into orbit around Saturn from vents in the moon's south polar region. According to Andrew Ingersoll, California Institute of Technology, "Other moons in the Solar System have liquid-water oceans covered by kilometers of icy crust. What's different here is that pockets of liquid water may be no more than tens of meters below the surface."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup> Over 100 geysers have been identified on Enceladus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140728_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140728-135">[133]</a></sup> In May 2011, NASA scientists at an Enceladus Focus Group Conference reported that Enceladus "is emerging as the most habitable spot beyond Earth in the Solar System for life as we know it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Cassini</i> photographs have led to other significant discoveries. They have revealed a previously undiscovered planetary ring, outside the brighter main rings of Saturn and inside the G and E rings. The source of this ring is hypothesized to be the crashing of a meteoroid off <a href="/wiki/Janus_(moon)" title="Janus (moon)">Janus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epimetheus_(moon)" title="Epimetheus (moon)">Epimetheus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup> In July 2006, <i>Cassini</i> images provided evidence of hydrocarbon lakes near Titan's north pole, the presence of which were confirmed in January 2007. In March 2007, additional images near Titan's north pole revealed hydrocarbon seas, the largest of which is almost the size of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup> In October 2006, the probe detected an 8,000 km diameter cyclone-like storm with an eyewall at Saturn's south pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 2004 to 2 November 2009, the probe discovered and confirmed eight new satellites. Its primary mission ended in 2008 when the spacecraft had completed 74 orbits around the planet. The probe's mission was extended to September 2010 and then extended again to 2017, to study a full period of Saturn's seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2013 <i>Cassini</i> sent back images of a hurricane at the planet's north pole 20 times larger than those found on Earth, with winds faster than 530 km/h.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 19 July 2013, "<a href="/wiki/The_Day_the_Earth_Smiled" title="The Day the Earth Smiled">The Day the Earth Smiled</a>", <i>Cassini</i> was pointed towards Earth to capture an image of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> (and, as well, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>) as part of a natural light, multi-image portrait of the entire Saturn system. It was the first time NASA informed the people of Earth that a long-distance photo was being taken in advance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20131112_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20131112-144">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 15 September 2017, the <i>Cassini-Huygens</i> spacecraft performed the "Grand Finale" of its mission: a number of passes through gaps between Saturn and Saturn's inner rings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20170915_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20170915-145">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170914_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170914-146">[144]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">atmospheric entry</a> of <i>Cassini</i> ended the mission.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_future_missions">Possible future missions</span></h4>
<p>The continued exploration of Saturn is still considered to be a viable option for NASA as part of their ongoing New Frontiers program of missions. NASA previously requested for plans to be put forward for a mission to Saturn that included an atmospheric entry probe and possible investigations into the habitability and possible discovery of life on Saturn's moons Titan and Enceladus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation">Observation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn-27-03-04.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="280" data-file-width="440" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Saturn-27-03-04.jpeg/220px-Saturn-27-03-04.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Saturn-27-03-04.jpeg/330px-Saturn-27-03-04.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/Saturn-27-03-04.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn-27-03-04.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Amateur telescopic view of Saturn</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Saturn is the most distant of the five planets easily visible to the naked eye from Earth, the other four being <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> and Jupiter. (Uranus and occasionally <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">4 Vesta</a> are visible to the naked eye in dark skies.) Saturn appears to the naked eye in the night sky as a bright, yellowish point of light with an <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> of usually between +1 and 0. It takes approximately 29.5 years for the planet to complete an entire circuit of the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a> against the background constellations of the <a href="/wiki/Zodiac" title="Zodiac">zodiac</a>. Most people will require an optical aid (very large binoculars or a small telescope) that magnifies at least 30 times to achieve an image of Saturn's rings, in which clear resolution is present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMM_Saturn_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-NMM_Saturn-31">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-binoculars_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-binoculars-123">[121]</a></sup> Twice every Saturnian year (roughly every 15 Earth years), the rings briefly disappear from view, due to the way in which they are angled and because they are so thin. Such a "disappearance" will next occur in 2025, but Saturn will be too close to the Sun for any ring-crossing observation to be possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturnoppositions-animated.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="112" data-file-width="175" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fe/Saturnoppositions-animated.gif" width="175"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturnoppositions-animated.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Simulated appearance of Saturn as seen from Earth (at opposition) during an orbit of Saturn, 2001-2029</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_eclipse.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1364" data-file-width="2766" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Saturn_eclipse.jpg/220px-Saturn_eclipse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Saturn_eclipse.jpg/330px-Saturn_eclipse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Saturn_eclipse.jpg/440px-Saturn_eclipse.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_eclipse.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Saturn eclipses the Sun, as seen from <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini</a></i>. The rings are visible, including the F Ring.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Saturn and its rings are best seen when the planet is at, or near, <a href="/wiki/Opposition_(planets)" title="Opposition (planets)">opposition</a>, the configuration of a planet when it is at an <a href="/wiki/Elongation_(astronomy)" title="Elongation (astronomy)">elongation</a> of 180°, and thus appears opposite the Sun in the sky. A Saturnian opposition occurs every year—approximately every 378 days—and results in the planet appearing at its brightest. Both the Earth and Saturn orbit the Sun on eccentric orbits, which means their distances from the Sun vary over time, and therefore so do their distances from each other, hence varying the brightness of Saturn from one opposition to the next. Saturn also appears brighter when the rings are angled such that they are more visible. For example, during the opposition of 17 December 2002, Saturn appeared at its brightest due to a favorable <a href="/wiki/File:Saturnoppositions.jpg" title="File:Saturnoppositions.jpg">orientation of its rings</a> relative to the Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-opp2002_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-opp2002-149">[147]</a></sup> even though Saturn was closer to the Earth and Sun in late 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-opp2002_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-opp2002-149">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>From time to time Saturn is <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occulted</a> by the Moon (that is, the Moon covers up Saturn in the sky). As with all the planets in the Solar System, occultations of Saturn occur in "seasons". Saturnian occultations will take place 12 or more times over a 12-month period, followed by about a five-year period in which no such activity is registered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jon_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jon-150">[148]</a></sup> Australian astronomy experts Hill and Horner explain the seasonal nature of Saturnian occultations:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>This is the result of the fact that the moon’s orbit around the Earth is tilted to the orbit of the Earth around the Sun – and so most of the time, the moon will pass above or below Saturn in the sky, and no occultation will occur. It is only when Saturn lies near the point that the moon’s orbit crosses the "plane of the ecliptic" that occultations can happen – and then they occur every time the moon swings by, until Saturn moves away from the crossing point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jon_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jon-150">[148]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">

<li id="cite_note-117"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-117">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.greek-names.info/greek-names-of-the-planets/" rel="nofollow">"Greek Names of the Planets"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">.
